        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/jacs.5b13273"><meta property="og:title" content="Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/jacs.5b13273.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Operando pair distribution function (PDF) analysis and ex situ 23Na magic-angle spinning solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (MAS ssNMR) spectroscopy are used to gain insight into the alloying mechanism of high-capacity antimony anodes for sodium-ion batteries. Subtraction of the PDF of crystalline NaxSb phases from the total PDF, an approach constrained by chemical phase information gained from 23Na ssNMR in reference to relevant model compounds, identifies two previously uncharacterized intermediate species formed electrochemically; a-Na3–xSb (x ≈ 0.4–0.5), a structure locally similar to crystalline Na3Sb (c-Na3Sb) but with significant numbers of sodium vacancies and a limited correlation length, and a-Na1.7Sb, a highly amorphous structure featuring some Sb–Sb bonding. The first sodiation breaks down the crystalline antimony to form first a-Na3–xSb and, finally, crystalline Na3Sb. Desodiation results in the formation of an electrode formed of a composite of crystalline and amorphous antimony networks. We link the different reactivity of these networks to a series of sequential sodiation reactions manifesting as a cascade of processes observed in the electrochemical profile of subsequent cycles. The amorphous network reacts at higher voltages reforming a-Na1.7Sb, then a-Na3–xSb, whereas lower potentials are required for the sodiation of crystalline antimony, which reacts to form a-Na3–xSb without the formation of a-Na1.7Sb. a-Na3–xSb is converted to crystalline Na3Sb at the end of the second discharge. We find no evidence of formation of NaSb. Variable temperature 23Na NMR experiments reveal significant sodium mobility within c-Na3Sb; this is a possible contributing factor to the excellent rate performance of Sb anodes."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/jacs.5b13273.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318af6d3abc88d3-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318af6d3abc88d3-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '510570d2-cef3-4fa1-ac17-37cc3a7c0c71'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318af6d3abc88d3-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'JACS';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/JACS/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/JACS/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="9318af6d3abc88d3-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "jacsat";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Tracking Sodium-Antimonide Phase Transformations in Sodium-Ion Anodes: Insights from Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis and Solid-State NMR Spectroscopy", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.5b13273", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

                        </p></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --><div class="articleMetrics articleMetrics--article"><div class="articleMetrics_table"><div class="articleMetrics_row"><input type="hidden" name="articleMetricsDoi" value="10.1021/jacs.5b13273"><div class="articleMetrics_metric"><h2>Article Views</h2><div tabindex="-1" aria-describedby="views-tooltip" class="articleMetrics-val articleMetrics-tooltip"><span>6852</span></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_altmetric"><h2>Altmetric</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val articleMetrics_score">1</div><div class="altmetric-donut hidden"><div data-badge-details="right" data-badge-type="donut" data-hide-no-mentions="false" data-condensed="true" class="altmetric-embed"></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">184</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base dropBlock"><a href="#" data-db-target-for="aboutArticleMetrics" aria-describedby="about-article-metrics" class="article_aboutLink">Learn about these metrics</a><div data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Operando pair distribution function (PDF) analysis and ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na magic-angle spinning solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance (MAS ssNMR) spectroscopy are used to gain insight into the alloying mechanism of high-capacity antimony anodes for sodium-ion batteries. Subtraction of the PDF of crystalline Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases from the total PDF, an approach constrained by chemical phase information gained from <sup>23</sup>Na ssNMR in reference to relevant model compounds, identifies two previously uncharacterized intermediate species formed electrochemically; a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb (<i>x</i> ≈ 0.4–0.5), a structure locally similar to crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) but with significant numbers of sodium vacancies and a limited correlation length, and a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb, a highly amorphous structure featuring some Sb–Sb bonding. The first sodiation breaks down the crystalline antimony to form first a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb and, finally, crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. Desodiation results in the formation of an electrode formed of a composite of crystalline and amorphous antimony networks. We link the different reactivity of these networks to a series of sequential sodiation reactions manifesting as a cascade of processes observed in the electrochemical profile of subsequent cycles. The amorphous network reacts at higher voltages reforming a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb, then a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb, whereas lower potentials are required for the sodiation of crystalline antimony, which reacts to form a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb without the formation of a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb. a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb is converted to crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>Sb at the end of the second discharge. We find no evidence of formation of NaSb. Variable temperature <sup>23</sup>Na NMR experiments reveal significant sodium mobility within c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb; this is a possible contributing factor to the excellent rate performance of Sb anodes.</p></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291757&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Antimony"> Antimony </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure"> Chemical structure </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291774&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy"> Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium"> Sodium </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) are of great interest as a complementary technology to lithium-ion batteries in applications such as grid-storage, where cost and sustainability are of greater importance than high energy density. However, graphite, the anode of choice for lithium-ion batteries, shows almost no capacity for sodium, meaning that the identification of suitable alternative anode materials is a major technological challenge. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Alloying anodes consisting of metals or metalloids that form alloys with sodium are of great interest due to their low operating voltage and high gravimetric capacities, reversible capacities of 480, 350, 500, and 580 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> having been reported for Pb, Ge, Sn and Sb, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 2 to 4">(2-4)</a> The performance of antimony at high rates sets it apart from other alloying materials; micrometric antimony can retain a capacity of 580 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> at a rate of C/6 and 520 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> at a rate of 3/4 C, something that has not been reported for lead, germanium or tin elemental anodes in NIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3, 5 and 6">(3, 5, 6)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Despite the advantages of alloying anodes, there are still major issues that need to be addressed if these systems are going to be used in real applications. For example, the large volume expansions that take place on sodiation can lead to harmful mechanical processes and side-reactions that cause capacity fade with cycling. This can be somewhat mediated by careful electrode formulation or nanocompositing the material with carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 7 to 11">(7-11)</a> More fundamental, however, is a lack of understanding of the phases that are formed within the battery and the transformation between them, which stems from the formation of highly reactive and often amorphous intermediate phases. Structural elucidation of these amorphous phases is challenging for conventional crystallographic methods, especially in complex systems where there is coexistence of amorphous and crystalline phases; the latter can overwhelm the analysis and obscure information about the relatively weakly scattering amorphous phases. Therefore, in order to obtain a full understanding of the electrochemical mechanism it is necessary to develop analyses that are able to extract the structure of these amorphous phases.</div><div class="NLM_p">In the case of antimony, numerous details of the (de)sodiation processes remain a mystery; the first sodiation is dominated by a single process at 500 mV, but subsequent (de)sodiations have strikingly different electrochemical profiles, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3, 5 and 6">(3, 5, 6)</a> suggesting that the structural transformations on the first and second sodiation are significantly different. This raises questions about the origin of this effect as well as what the structure of the electrode at the top of charge is, as this effectively becomes the “active” electrode for all future cycles. The intermediate phases that are formed during cycling are also unknown; X-ray diffraction (XRD) studies confirm that crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) is formed at the end of both the first and second sodiation, but other than the formation of this crystalline phase, the electrode is amorphous for large sections of the first sodiation and all of the subsequent (de)sodiation processes, meaning XRD is unable to probe their structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> There is no clear evidence from XRD that NaSb, the only other sodium antimonide in the known Na–Sb phase diagram is formed at any stage in the (de)sodiation process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Baggetto et al. probed thin film electrodes in the first sodiation and first desodiation using <sup>119</sup>Sb Mossbauer spectroscopy and proposed that Sb environments similar to those found in NaSb are formed during both processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> However, the close similarity between the isomeric shifts of the NaSb structure and those arising from a combination of Sb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb do not allow for an unequivocal conclusion about the nature of this intermediate phase.</div><div class="NLM_p">Given these complexities, it is clear that further structural elucidation is required in the intermediate regions of the (de)sodiation of antimony, with a focus on techniques that are able to isolate and characterize any amorphous or disordered intermediates and track their interconversion, especially in the presence of additional crystalline phases. Pair distribution function (PDF) analysis uses Bragg scattering and diffuse scattering in parallel, meaning that while any long-range order that is present in the material is taken into account, it is also possible to characterize structures with deviations from long-range order, or phases that lack long-range order altogether. The method has recently found application in characterization of battery electrodes both ex situ and during operation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 14 to 22">(14-22)</a> Operando measurements, where scattering data is collected during battery operation, have particular advantages over post-mortem analysis as they increase the consistency between data sets and circumvent issues of air contamination, which can complicate analysis; this is particularly relevant to the very air sensitive alloy samples considered here. In addition to these factors, operando measurements can capture the formation of metastable species that may form in the battery but may relax before post-mortem analysis is carried out. Visual examination of the PDF can give direct structural information, but additional quantitative information can be extracted by using atomistic modeling approaches, such as real-space Rietveld, reverse Monte Carlo methods and empirical potential structure refinements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 to 25">(23-25)</a> However, one of the challenges of modeling the PDF of battery systems is the separation of amorphous and crystalline phases when present simultaneously. To obtain unambiguous interpretation, it is necessary to add additional chemical information about the number and nature of phases. Furthermore, while X-ray PDF is highly sensitive to the components of the electrode that are strongly scattering—in this case meaning that changes to the antimony connectivity during sodium insertion are very distinct—the contribution of sodium to the PDF is relatively small.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Here, we present a pair distribution function and high-resolution <sup>23</sup>Na magic-angle spinning solid-state nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy (MAS ssNMR) study of antimony anodes for sodium-ion batteries. We focus on using in-depth analysis of PDF data, constrained by chemical information from <sup>23</sup>Na ssNMR experiments referenced to synthesized model compounds of the two known sodium-antimonide compounds NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, in order to isolate amorphous and crystalline phases present in the electrode, and understand their transformations during the (de)sodiation process. Through this approach, we are able to propose structural origins for each of the distinct electrochemical signatures, and rationalize the different electrochemical profile observed on the second sodiation process.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Preparation of Model Compounds NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Stoichiometric ratios of Sb powder (Sigma-Aldrich, 99.99%) and sodium metal (Sigma-Aldrich, &gt;99%) were ball milled using a hardened stainless steel ball-mill jar in a planetary Ball Mill PM 100 under Ar atmosphere. An active-milling time of 60 min was sufficient to obtain a complete reaction of Sb and Na. The formation of NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb was confirmed using XRD (See <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec5" aria-label="Section 5">Section </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec5" aria-label="5">5</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S10).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Formulation of Electrodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Antimony powder (−200 mesh, 99.99%, Sigma-Aldrich) was ball milled under argon for 30 min using a SPEX 8000 M high-energy ball mill. Electrodes were formulated by forming an aqueous slurry using a 70:18:12 ratio of Sb, carboxymethylcellulose (CMC) (DS = 0.7, <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 250 000, Sigma-Aldrich), SuperP carbon (Timcal). The slurry was ball-milled in a Retsch PM100 plenary ball-mill for 1 h, before being cast on a glass slide using a 150 μm doctor blade. The film was dried for 16 h in air, and dried at 110 °C under a vacuum for 2 h.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 PDF Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">Ex situ PDF samples were packed into 0.0485 in. diameter Kapton capillaries (Cole-Parmer), and sealed with epoxy in an argon atmosphere. Operando PDF measurements were made using the “AMPIX” electrochemical cell. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Cells were assembled using Na-metal (Sigma, &gt;99%) as the counter electrode, 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> (99.5+, Sigma) in PC (Anhydrous, Sigma-Aldrich) as the electrolyte and a glass-fiber separator (Whatman, GF/A) in an argon atmosphere glovebox. Cells were cycled galvanostatically at a rate of C/20 (based on mass of antimony) in the range of 2.5 to 0 V, where C/20 corresponds to the insertion of 3 Na per Sb in 20 h. X-ray total scattering data were collected using 45 min intervals, corresponding to the insertion of 0.1125 Na, with an exposure time of 180 s. Data were collected for the first sodiation, first desodiation, and second sodiation. The cycling rate and number of discharge–charge cycles were ultimately limited by allocated beamtime.</div><div class="NLM_p">X-ray scattering data were collected at 11-ID-B at the Advanced Photon Source, Argonne National Laboratory using an X-ray energy of 86.7 keV (λ = 0.1430 Å) and an amorphous silicon area detector (PerkinElmer) to obtain data to large momentum transfer values. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26, 27)</a> Data were integrated using the program Fit2D, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> standard corrections (background, Compton scattering, detector effects) were applied, and the data Fourier transformed (<i>Q</i><sub>max</sub> = 18 Å) to obtain <i>G</i>(<i>r</i>) using the software PDFGetX2. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Structural models were refined against PDF data using PDFGui, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> using previously published structures from the ICSD database. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> For the refinement of any crystalline phases present within the electrode, starting values for the structural models were taken from the refinement of model compounds for Sb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. If the <i>r</i>-factor (<i>R</i><sub><i>w</i></sub>) for the refinement was unsatisfactory, an additional minor phase was added and the relative scale parameters were refined. When the minor phase was present in small quantities (when the scale factor was less than 10% of the scale factor of the phase when present as a single-phase electrode, i.e., at the start of sodiation for c-Sb or the end of sodiation for c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) thermal parameters were fixed to values representative of those in regions where the phase was the major constituent of the electrode. A spherical particle envelope was used to model the particle size and/or length scale of ordering in areas where phases were present with limited correlations. <i>R</i><sub><i>w</i></sub> values from the final refinements are quoted to give an idea of the attainable fit and to allow the competing models to be compared. <i>R</i><sub><i>w</i></sub> for PDF refinements is generally higher than those typically found an XRD Rietveld refinement, which reflects the fact that the functions being fit are different, with <i>G</i>(<i>r</i>) being more sensitive to the local atomic structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Residuals of greater than 15% are common even in well-ordered materials. <i>R</i><sub><i>w</i></sub> remains, however, useful as a measure of goodness-of-fit when comparing models fit to PDF data.</div><div class="NLM_p">To extract information about additional phases with limited correlation lengths, a refinement for the crystalline phases present was performed at high-<i>r</i> (20–50 Å) using the values for low-<i>r</i> peak sharpening fixed to values determined from refinements where each the crystalline phase was dominant. The refinement was extended to the full <i>r</i>-range (2–50 Å) keeping all parameters fixed to examine the contribution of the crystalline phase to the whole PDF. The contribution of amorphous components was assessed from the residual (<i>G</i>(<i>r</i>)<sub>experiment</sub> – <i>G</i>(<i>r</i>)<sub>model</sub>) of this refinement. From the onset of desodiation, the PDFs obtained have a small contribution from sodium metal, due to additional texture on the counter electrode that could not be accounted for during background subtraction. This was modeled as an additional phase with fixed unit cell parameters and thermal parameters. The contribution of this phase to the PDF as a function of sodiation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>) was found to be small in all data sets compared to the contribution from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases. Additional amorphous phases were added to the refinements for the first sodiation process using a model that was refined against data from the second sodiation (at the end of the D2-c process), where the amorphous phase is assumed to be phase-pure. In these multiphase refinements, only the scale factors for the three phases were refined.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The sodiation level, defined here as the number of sodium atoms per antimony was calculated from structural data by considering the distribution of antimony between the phases present (as output from refinements in PDFGui) and the number of sodiums per antimony in each phase (e.g., 0 for c-Sb, 3 for Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, 2.5 for a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb). For the electrochemical measurements, the number of Na per Sb was calculated from the current that was passed; 0.1125 Na per Sb were added between each data set at the current used. Details of the calculations for the sodiation level of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases are shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.4 NMR Spectroscopy</h3><div class="NLM_p">Sb/C/CMC films (6–12 mg) were assembled in 2032 coin cells using a fiber-glass separator (Whatman) and sodium metal (Sigma-Aldrich, &gt;99%) as the counter electrode. 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> (Sigma-Aldrich, &gt;99.5%) in PC (Sigma-Aldrich, anhydrous) was used as the electrolyte. Cells were cycled at a rate of C/20 using an Arbin Instruments electrochemical cycler, to points of interest in the electrochemical curve. The batteries were dissembled inside an argon glovebox, and the active material washed with dimethylcarbonate (Sigma-Aldrich, anhydrous, &gt;99%) in order to remove excess electrolyte from the sample. Samples were dried under a vacuum, before being packed into kel-F inserts placed in 4 mm zirconia rotors and closed with kel-F caps inside an argon glovebox. Though the formation of decomposition products of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases (e.g., NaOH) was minimized by the careful handling of materials in an argon atmosphere, it is possible that small quantities remain.</div><div class="NLM_p last"><sup>23</sup>Na solid-state NMR data were collected on a Bruker AVIII-700 spectrometer working at a <sup>23</sup>Na Larmor frequency of 185.2 MHz. Rotors were spun at magic-angle spinning (MAS) rates between 5 kHz and 10 kHz. Spectra were acquired using a pulse-acquire sequence, with a 90° pulse duration of 2.75 μs and a recycle interval of 5 s. Between 2000 and 10 000 transients were coadded for each spectrum, depending on the mass of the sample in the rotor, and level of sodiation. Spectra in the plots are normalized with respect to the sample weight and the number of scans used in the experiment. <sup>23</sup>Na chemical shifts are referenced relative to solid NaCl at 7.2 ppm.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3 Results</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">3.1 Structural Characterization of Model Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p">Prior to the study of Sb battery anodes, structural characterization was carried out for crystalline NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb reference materials. Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (space group 194, <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>) is formed of hexagonal layers of alternating Na and Sb atoms with additional sodium ions residing between the layers (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, top left). There are two sodium environments in a 1:2 ratio; sodium within the hexagonal layers sits in a trigonal environment, and sodium between the layers is in a tetrahedral environment. NaSb (space group 14, <i>P</i>2<sub>1</sub>/<i>c</i>) is formed of interlinked helical chains of Sb and Na running parallel to the <i>b</i>-axis (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, bottom left). The two sodium environments are present in a 1:1 ratio.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0010.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Left: unit cell of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (top) and 1 × 2 × 1 unit cells of NaSb showing the helical chains of antimony (bottom). Antimony is shown in blue. For Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, Na1 is shown in green and Na2 in orange. For NaSb, the sodium is shown in orange. (b) Middle: <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra of synthesized Na<sub>3</sub>Sb at 268 K (top), Na<sub>3</sub>Sb at 298 K (middle) and NaSb (bottom), all recorded at 10 kHz MAS with an external field of 16.4 T. Chemical shifts of major isotropic resonances are marked. * = spinning sidebands. (c) Right: least-squared refinement of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (top) and NaSb (bottom) structures against ex situ PDF data. Experimental data is shown as symbols, green lines shows the calculated PDF. The difference between the experimental and model PDFs is shown by the black line, offset for clarity. A 7% Sb impurity (by mass) is present in the NaSb sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra for the two model compounds are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (middle). For Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, the spectrum recorded at 298 K shows a sharp feature with a poorly resolved shoulder. In a second spectrum recorded at 268 K, the observed features are resolved into a sharp resonance at 52 ppm together with a clearly defined quadrupolar line shape centered around 65 ppm. Fitting of the two resonances yields NMR parameters δ<sub>iso</sub> = 52 ppm, <i>C</i><sub>Q</sub> = 0 MHz, η<sub>Q</sub> = 0.15, (ascribed to the tetrahedral Na1 site between the layers) and δ<sub>iso</sub> = 83 ppm, <i>C</i><sub>Q</sub> = 4.6 MHz, η<sub>Q</sub> = 0.06 (ascribed to the trigonal Na2 site within the layers). The fit of simulated peaks to the experimental spectrum for Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12 and Table S5</a>. The large temperature dependence observed in the spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>) indicates that there is significant exchange between the two sodium sites in the structure, occurring even at room temperature; simulations of the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra allow the exchange frequency between sodium sites to be estimated as around 1–2 kHz at room temperature. The <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectrum of NaSb exhibits two partially resolved features at 18 and 22 ppm. A multiple-quantum MAS (MQMAS) spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>) confirms that these correspond to two sites, consistent with the two crystallographic sites present in the NaSb structure, both with very small quadrupolar parameters.</div><div class="NLM_p">The <sup>23</sup>Na NMR results demonstrate that the Sb-connectivity has a clear effect on the <sup>23</sup>Na shift, something that has been observed in lithium-alloy electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 22 and 32 to 34">(22, 32-34)</a> In the case of Na–Sb, a change from Sb–Sb chains (as in NaSb) to units of isolated Sb ions (as in Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) moves the resonances to higher chemical shift. The opposite trend is observed in lithium-alloys (Li–Si, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Li–Ge, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Li–Sb <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a>), highlighting potential differences in the electronic structure of sodium phases. The shifts observed for these reference compounds will be used to help interpret the spectra for electrochemically sodiated Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases. Real-space least-squared refinements using the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and NaSb structures reported by Brauer et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> and Cromer et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> show a good match to the experimental PDF data for these compounds indicating that there is a good agreement of the local and long-range structure. Refinement plots and structural data are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> (right). Of note, is the large value of the U<sub>33</sub> thermal displacement parameter obtained from the refinement for the Na2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>), the sodium-ions within the hexagonal layers of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, corresponding to displacements in the c-direction, normal to the hexagonal layers of the material. This implies that there is some disorder in the height/position of the in plane sodium ions, which may come about through the significant site exchange observed by <sup>23</sup>Na NMR.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In order to assess the contribution of sodium within the CMC binder and conductive carbon to the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra for the battery samples, <sup>23</sup>Na NMR experiments for the pristine electrode and for sample of Super P and CMC in the ratio 40:60 discharged to 0 V with the same effective current as the antimony samples were performed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>). The CMC binder in the pristine electrode gives a broad resonance between −40 and 20 ppm. The spectra for the discharged Super P carbon/CMC sample is dominated by this resonance and by resonances between 0 and 20 ppm from sodium contained in the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI), on the surface of the electrode, and that reacts with the conductive carbon additive, as well as from any residual electrolyte that was not removed by washing.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.2 In Operando PDF and Ex Situ <sup>23</sup>Na NMR Studies of Sb Anodes</h3><div id="sec3_2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2.1 Galvanostatic Electrochemical Measurements</h4><div class="NLM_p">The galvanostatic (constant current) electrochemical profile and the differential capacity (dQ/dV) for antimony, shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>, are similar to those reported previously. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3, 5 and 6">(3, 5, 6)</a> The first sodiation process is dominated by a flat plateau at around 500 mV (S1-a) before a voltage drop to 0 V (S1-b), corresponding to the addition of slightly more than 3 Na per Sb. During desodiation, approximately 2.5 Na per Sb are removed from the electrode, corresponding to 82% of the theoretical capacity. The desodiation processes shows a significant voltage hysteresis and features three processes: a distinct plateau at 800 mV (D1-a) followed by a sloping process at 850–950 mV (D1-b) and a sharp rise to 2 V (D1-c). Subsequent sodiations show three processes; a short plateau at 750–650 mV (S2-a), a sloping process to 450 mV (S2-b) and at least one additional plateau at 450 mV (S2-c), before dropping to 0 V (S2-d). The relatively large Sb-particle size means that no significant capacity from the reaction of surface antimony oxides, which is expected to occur primarily above 500 mV, is observed. Therefore, this represents a “clean” model system for studying only the antimony phase transformations, but is also highly relevant to nanostructured antimony (e.g., nanoparticles or Sb/C composites) where similar electrochemical processes are observed beyond the first sodiation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 to 9">(6-9)</a> Note that the chief difference between nanostructured antimony and bulk antimony in the first (de)sodiation cycle is the additional irreversible capacity, which can be attributed to increased SEI formation on the higher surface area electrodes, and the increased quantities of surface antimony oxides. Electrochemical data for subsequent cycles for bulk antimony is similar and has been reported previously. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref6" aria-label="reference 3 and 6">(3, 6)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Top: (De)sodiation curves obtained for antimony vs sodium metal cycled at a rate of C/20 in the voltage range of 2.5 to 0 V. The different electrochemical processes are labeled and these labels referred to subsequently in the text. Bottom: dQ/dV plots for 1st and 2nd cycle. The different electrochemical processes are marked with a notation that is used throughout the subsequent text.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.2.2 Ex Situ <sup>23</sup>Na NMR</h4><div class="NLM_p">Ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na MAS ssNMR provides insight into the sodium local environments as a function of (de)sodiation (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>, left). At a low sodiation level of 0.6 Na per Sb the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra are dominated by resonances between −40 and 20 ppm from sodium within the binder, the conductive carbon and the SEI as discussed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec3_1" aria-label="Section 3.1">Section </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec3_1" aria-label="3.1">3.1</a>. On further sodiation (&gt;1 Na per Sb), an additional broad peak is observed in the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR, centered around 37 ppm. It is clear that this shift is different to those observed for both NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, and indicates that the sodium exists in environments where the antimony connectivity is intermediate between NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. During the second half of S1-a, the resonance at 37 ppm grows and shifts gradually on increasing sodiation to 42 ppm. Additional weak resonances between 50 and 80 ppm are observed, corresponding to the two sites of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, clearly indicating the coexistence of multiple Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases in this region. During S1-b, the additional phase is converted to c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb; the 37–42 ppm resonance becomes weaker and the c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb resonances dominate the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra. The peaks from c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb show a similar broadening to the model Na<sub>3</sub>Sb compound indicating that similar Na exchange processes are present within the electrode. The final nominal composition of the sample at the end of sodiation (EoS) is Na<sub>3.375</sub>Sb, indicating that 0.375 Na has been consumed in side reactions to form the SEI.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra (normalized) of cycled Sb electrodes at the states of charge. Spectra were recorded at 10 kHz MAS with an external field of 16.4 T. Chemical shifts of major isotropic resonances are marked. The shaded region marks where resonances from sodium within the CMC binder, the SEI and the conductive carbon are dominant. * mark spinning sidebands. Number (#) of sodiums per antimony is labeled next to each spectra, based on the calculations outlined in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, EOS = end of sodiation, EOD = end of desodiation. Alternate lines are dashed for clarity. (b) Discharge–charge curves obtained for Sb during the in situ PDF measurements. (c) Selected PDFs obtained during the first discharge, first charge and second discharge cycles by Fourier transforming the total scattering X-ray data. PDF are vertically offset in time. The colors of the curves correspond to the colors of the points on the electrochemical curve in (b) where the samples were extracted for NMR/PDF analyses.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">On desodiation, <sup>23</sup>Na NMR resonances from c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb lose intensity during D1-a. The resonance at 37 ppm from a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb does not reappear, confirming that a different reaction pathway is taken on desodiation. Instead, a very broad resonance centered at 27 ppm appears, indicative of sodium being within a phase with a higher Sb–Sb connectivity than Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, and ruling out the formation of NaSb. At the end of desodiation, the only strong peaks in the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra originate from sodium with the conductive carbon, the SEI and the CMC binder.</div><div class="NLM_p">Approximately 1 sodium per antimony remains in the electrode at the end of desodiation (EoD), at least some of which are likely present in the SEI (approximately 0.375 Na per Sb based on the additional capacity observed on sodiation). However, based on the consistency of capacity measurements between electrode formulations and experimental setups in this study and previous studies (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), it is likely that at least some of the sodium remains within the connected Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases of the electrode, but in concentrations too low to give rise to distinct resonances in the region dominated by sodium contained in the SEI and the CMC binder. Certainly, the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra indicate that very little c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb remains in the electrode at the end of desodiation, indicating that significant loss of electrical contact does not take place during the D-1a/b processes, suggesting that if any sodium antimonide remains, it does so within the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases.</div><div class="NLM_p">During the second sodiation, both intermediate species and c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb are reformed; the resonance at 27 ppm is the dominant Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phase at the end of S2-a, and grows in intensity during S2-b. During S2-c resonances at both 37 ppm and from c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb appear, and at the end of the second sodiation, c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is present in isolation.</div><div class="NLM_p last">These results demonstrate that sodium environments consistent with c-NaSb are not formed during (de)sodiation; instead two other Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb intermediates species, characterized by broad <sup>23</sup>Na resonances at ∼37 ppm and ∼27 ppm are formed. In the following sections, the structure and interconversion of these phases is analyzed in the context of operando PDF measurements, constrained by the information from these <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra.</div></div><div id="sec3_2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.2.3 In Operando Pair Distribution Function Analysis</h4><div class="NLM_p">PDFs obtained as a function of sodiation are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> (right), alongside the electrochemical profile for the galvanostatic (constant current) measurement during the in situ PDF measurements (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>, middle). Full data are shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Least-squares refinement of the structure of crystalline hexagonal antimony (c-Sb) against PDF data for the pristine electrode shows a good fit (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17, Table S3</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> A nearest neighbor peak at 2.85 Å is characteristic of Sb–Sb bonds within the puckered hexagonal layers; a longer correlation at 3.3 Å is a signature of the weaker between-layer correlations. No attempt was made to add contributions from the CMC binder or conductive carbon to the refinement, because these are weak X-ray scatterers with limited correlations lengths, so are unlikely to contribute strongly to the PDF. No significant peaks are observed in the residual from the one-phase PDF refinement for the pristine material, confirming that the contribution of other electrode components is minimal.</div><div id="sec3_2_3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_4"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h5 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.2.3.1 First Sodiation</h5><div class="NLM_p">Early in S1-a, up to a sodiation level of 0.5 Na per Sb, the PDF shows relatively little change, confirming the utilization of sodium inserted in this region in the formation of light species in the SEI or reaction with surface species and the conductive carbon to which the PDF is relatively insensitive. Upon further sodiation, the large observed changes to the PDF are an indication that the structure of the electrode undergoes drastic changes, as is expected for an alloying mechanism. The high-<i>r</i> peaks lose intensity as the long-range structure of crystalline Sb is broken down but strong peaks remain at low-<i>r</i> indicating that some degree of local-order remains. While the amount of crystalline material present in the material decreases during S1-a, those present can be modeled well through a two-phase refinement using the c-Sb and c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb structure against PDF data in the distance range of 20–50 Å, i.e., the distance where the crystalline phases will dominate; good fits are obtained in this distance range for all data sets with no significant peaks remaining in the residual, indicating no further crystalline phases are formed, in agreement with previous studies. During S1-a, c-Sb is the dominant crystalline phase present up to a Na:Sb ratio of approximately 1.7:1, albeit with a steady decrease in the scale factor for this phase (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>); no significant quantity of c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is present (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S7</a>). The absence of sodium-containing crystalline phases results in a large discrepancy between the level of sodiation determined from electrochemical measurements and that calculated from the phase fractions of the crystalline phases present (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>, top) meaning additional amorphous phases must account for a significant amount of the sodium present in the electrode.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Comparison of the sodiation calculated from electrochemical measurements and from the phase fractions determined from least-squares refinements of PDF data. The dashed line shows the expected sodiation from electrochemical measurements. Blue triangles show the sodiation calculated from a two-phase fit using c-Sb and c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb in the distance range (20–50 Å); green crosses show the sodiation calculated from a two-phase fit using c-Sb and c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb in the distance range (2–50 Å); red circles show the sodiation calculated from a three-phase refinement using c-Sb, c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb. Details of calculations are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2_3" aria-label="section 2.3">section </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2_3" aria-label="2.3">2.3</a>. Bottom: Real-space least-squares refinements against PDF data at an electrode stoichiometry of Na<sub>2.36</sub>Sb during the first sodiation. (b) A two phase refinement in the range 2–50 Å. (c) A constrained refinement where the values obtained during a refinement in the distance range 20–50 Å were fixed for the refinement in the full distance range. (d) Three phase refinement using c-Sb, c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb (<i>x</i> = 0.5). For all refinements, experimental data is shown in gray circles, the fit to the data in orange, and the residual in gray (raw) or black (<i>r</i>-averaged over termination ripples). This residual is shown offset for clarity. Details of the calculations used to estimate error bars on the sodiation levels are shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">When two-phase refinements are extended to the full distance range (2–50 Å), the fit is unsatisfactory (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>(b)); at low-<i>r</i> there is significant mismatch between the model and the PDF data, and the fit at high-<i>r</i> is worse compared to the refinements against high-<i>r</i> PDF data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>), providing further evidence for the presence of additional phases in the system. In order to extract the PDF for these amorphous phases, the structural parameters for refinements at high-<i>r</i> (20–50 Å) were fixed and used to constrain a full <i>r</i>-range (2–50 Å) refinement against the PDF data. The residual (<i>G</i>(<i>r</i>)<sub>experiment</sub> – <i>G</i>(<i>r</i>)<sub>model</sub>) from these constrained refinements were then extracted as a function of sodiation level and represent the PDF of the amorphous phase. The differential PDF for the amorphous phase was calculated by subtracting the residual for the pristine electrode from subsequent residuals. This is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a> for process S1-a. Peaks in this residual PDF correspond to the additional interactions within the amorphous phase(s) in the system when sodium is added to the electrode. Strong peaks are observed at 3.1 and 5.4 Å, along with broader peaks out to 20 Å, but no high-<i>r</i> peaks are seen (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). The positions of the low-<i>r</i> peaks are close to the Na–Sb and Sb–Sb nearest correlations in c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (the PDF for both of these phases is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a), suggesting local environments similar to c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb are formed during S1-a, but without the long-range correlations present in a crystalline material. This is consistent with large peak width of the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra in this region, indicating a highly disordered structure in which the sodium is likely to have a range of coordination numbers and geometries.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. PDFs for the a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb intermediate formed during S1-a as a function of sodiation.
