Phase fractions for the crystalline phases are obtained from a two phase (c-Sb and c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) refinements against data at high-<i>r</i> (20–50 Å). These phase fractions were then fixed for refinements against data over the full <i>r</i>-range. The residual of these refinements represents the additional amorphous phases present in the electrode. The positions of the major peaks are marked. The electrochemical profile of the first sodiation is shown below; the color of the curve corresponds to the point on the electrochemical curve designated by the dot of that color.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) PDFs for the amorphous phases formed during (de)sodiation of antimony extracted from experimental data: a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb extracted from the amorphous component of the PDF at the end of D1-b; a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb extracted from the amorphous component of the PDF at the end of D1-a; a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb is the PDF extracted from the end of S2-c. Distinguishing distances for each of the phases are marked on the PDFs and calculated PDFs for c-Sb and c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (scaled by 0.5) are shown for comparison. (b) An Sb–Sb offset dumbbell arrangement giving rise to peaks in the same positions as the PDF for a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb. (c) Comparison the arrangement of Sb in c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (left) and c-Sb (right) looking down the <i>c</i>-axis; sodium is shown in orange, antimony is in blue. Light blue atoms lie in one plane, dark blue lie offset in another plane.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A comparison between the Sb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb structure (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c) shows that the puckered hexagonal arrangement of Sb atoms is retained in Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, with the insertion of additional sodium both between the Sb atoms within layers and between the layers, representing a kinetically facile pathway for sodiation. Therefore, we propose that the amorphous structure formed initially on sodiation is related to these other structures, with the Sb-interlayer distances reduced compared to c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, probably due to vacancies between the layers. On the basis of the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR shift, we propose that this phase—referred to herein as a-Na<sub>3–x</sub>Sb—is undersodiated compared to c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. Using data collected at a total electrode stoichiometry of Na<sub>2.7</sub>Sb where the PDF indicates that a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb is the major phase in the electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>), we estimate the stoichiometry of a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb as <i>x</i> = 0.4 by subtracting the contribution of sodium in the c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and within the SEI, estimated to be 0.78 and 0.375 Na per Sb, respectively, from the sodiation level calculated from the electrochemistry. Full details of these calculations are shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. Some deviation from this value is likely due to the difficulty in determining when in the electrochemical process the sodium consumed in side reactions (SEI, carbon surface reactions, etc.). However, further evidence of this under-sodiation comes from the second sodiation, where this amorphous a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb phase is reformed in isolation at the end of S2-c (see <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec3_2_3" aria-label="Section 3.2.3">Section </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec3_2_3" aria-label="3.2.3">3.2.3</a>), at an electrode stoichiometry of Na<sub>2.5</sub>Sb (calculated from electrochemical measurements), making <i>x</i> = 0.5 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a> compares the PDFs of these phases). Alloy phases are known to accommodate some degree of over- or under-stoichiometry, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref32 ref33 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref32 ref33 ref40" aria-label="reference 22, 32, 33 and 40">(22, 32, 33, 40)</a> there is likely to be some stoichiometric flexibility in this highly amorphous phase.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Above a sodiation level of 2.9 Na per Sb no further crystalline Sb is observed in the electrode; all the Sb–Sb bonds have now been broken. During S1-b, the crystallization of c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is observed; sharp high-<i>r</i> peaks corresponding to c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase appear in the PDF, and at the end of discharge, PDF data can be modeled well by using the crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>Sb structure, reported by Brauer and Zintl <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21, Table S4</a>) in agreement with other studies that report this as the final sodiation product. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 and 5">(3, 5)</a> No crystalline cubic Na<sub>3</sub>Sb was observed in contrast to a previous study that reported a minor amount of this high-pressure metastable phase alongside hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>Sb prior to the full crystallization of hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> The same large U<sub>33</sub> values observed for the Na2 site of the model compounds are also observed here. When linked with the <sup>23</sup>Na ssNMR, these results imply that Na<sub>3</sub>Sb has some degree of local disorder resulting in exchange of sodium between sites, which is not captured in the average structures of the previous reports.</div></div><div id="sec3_2_3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_4"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h5 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">3.2.3.2 First Desodiation</h5><div class="NLM_p">c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is broken down during D1-a, the high-<i>r</i> peaks disappearing while an additional interaction at 2.85 Å starts to appear. The additional interactions, again extracted from the residual of a constrained refinements against c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>(a) (middle) for the end of D1-a at an electrode stoichiometry of Na<sub>1.875</sub>Sb. The growth of this component during D1-a is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. It is immediately obvious that no antimony connectivity similar to a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb is present, confirming that a different reaction pathway is taken on desodiation. Instead, an additional phase is formed during D1-a, of approximate stoichiometry Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb (full details of these calculations are shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The structure is highly amorphous, with no peaks observed above 10 Å, consistent with the broad peak observed in the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR for this phase, which indicates highly disordered sodium environments.</div><div class="NLM_p">There is a single, sharp, intense peak at 2.85 Å, a signature of Sb–Sb bonding, along with weak, broad peaks centered at 4.6, 6.6, and 9.3 Å. The weak X-ray scattering power of Na in comparison to Sb, makes the presence of significant Na−Sb or Na−Na correlations in the PDF unlikely and therefore all peaks observed in the PDF are likely to come from Sb–Sb correlations. The sharpness of the first peak compared to the other peaks in the PDF indicate that the local Sb units are well-defined, probably as Sb–Sb dumbbells. Any more extended Sb–Sb connectivity (e.g., chains) would results in additional sharp peaks in the PDF at higher-<i>r</i> that are not observed here. Dumbbells are known to be stable structural motifs in other alkali alloy phases such as Li–Si, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref39 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33 ref39 ref41" aria-label="reference 32, 33, 39 and 41">(32, 33, 39, 41)</a> Li–Ge, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref32" aria-label="reference 22 and 32">(22, 32)</a> Li–Sn. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 10, 42 and 43">(10, 42, 43)</a> Certainly, the PDF in this region rules out the formation of NaSb that is postulated by Baggetto et al. because the weak peaks between 4 and 10 Å are not consistent with the structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a>) and the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra does not show the presence of this phase. A structure containing NaSb-like helical chains disordered in relation to one another is also ruled out as the peak positions do not match those observed experimentally (a calculated PDF is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a>). In contrast, a simple geometrical argument suggests that dumbbells, offset with respect to each other to form a parallelogram of Sb-atoms (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>(b)) would give peak positions at the correct distances and approximately the correct intensities. It should be noted, however, that the structure has a very high degree of disorder beyond the well-defined first nearest-neighbor distance, and the atomic arrangement presented here is only a representation of the local structural motifs present; no order on the long-range exists.</div><div class="NLM_p">The PDFs during D1-a can be reproduced well using a linear combination of the PDFs of c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, Figure S25), and the extracted phase fractions indicate a two-phase reaction between c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb during D1-a. At the end of D1-a, the PDF is a mixture of a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb and c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, where approximately 14% of the c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase remains, based on the relative scale factor obtained by least-squares refinement against PDF data.</div><div class="NLM_p">During D1-b, the remaining c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is broken down and there is a growth in intensity and change to the positions of low-<i>r</i> peaks, indicating both a growth of the amount of amorphous phase, and structural rearrangements within the amorphous phase. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> (top) shows the PDF for the phase formed in this region, the Na-content at this point corresponding to a stoichiometry of Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb (for the calculations of the sodiation level of this phase, see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The peak at 2.9 Å remains, a signature of the prevalence of Sb–Sb bonding, while the intensity of the 6.6 Å peak increases relative to that observed for a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb and shows no change in position. Larger changes occur between these distances; the peak at 4.6 Å is no longer evident, but two new peaks at 3.7 and 4.3 Å can be deconvoluted from the PDF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23</a>). These additional strong peaks at low-<i>r</i> indicate that a more highly connected antimony network results from the removal of sodium. The PDF for this structure shows a striking resemblance to a PDF reported for amorphous “explosive” antimony synthesized by electrolysis of antimony chloride by Krebs et al., who propose a structural model based on a three-connected random network of antimony, where all antimony are bonded to three other antimony atoms. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> A large range of dihedral angles result in the broadening of peaks beyond the first nearest neighbor and no long-range order to atom–atom correlations. No peak is observed at 3.3 Å, where the correlation between the puckered layers in c-Sb is observed. This disordered antimony phase is believed to have heteroatoms terminating Sb-fragments–in the case of the sample of Krebs, this is likely to be residual chloride. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Within the electrode, residual sodium or organic components from the decomposition of the organic electrolyte are likely to remain. We postulate that the lower density resulting from the lack of layering in the material compared to c-Sb could accommodate the residual sodium present at this composition. The residual sodium is likely to be highly disordered, broadening out the already weak contribution to the PDF, meaning that no clear Na–Na or Na–Sb interactions are observed in the PDF.</div><div class="NLM_p">During D1-c, peaks at high-<i>r</i> appear; these are modeled well by c-Sb (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S26, Table S8</a>) indicating that removal of further sodium from Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb results in the crystallization of some areas of the electrode, consistent with previous XRD report by Darwiche et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> The correlation length of this c-Sb at the end of charge is limited to 20 nm. It is clear, however, that a significant amount of the a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb remains in the electrode; a comparison of the scale factors for the c-Sb at the end of D1-c and for the pristine electrode estimates that 40% of the Sb is present as c-Sb, 60% of the antimony is present as the a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb network. The ratio of the first peaks in the PDF—assumed to be as a result of each Sb bonding to 3 other Sb atoms—for the amorphous component (extracted from the residual of PDF refinements carried out the <i>r</i>-range of 20–50 Å) and the crystalline component is 7:3, again estimating that 70% of the antimony remains as a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p last">At the end of desodiation, approximately 0.64 Na per Sb have not been removed in addition to the 0.375 Na per Sb that have been assigned to SEI formation during the first sodiation, either because of the loss of connection of areas of the electrode or because of sodium trapped in the electrode. We propose that loss of connection is unlikely, given the reproducibility of the effect in multiple electrodes and also in reports by other authors (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 3, 5 and 6">(3, 5, 6)</a> and, because the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR results indicate that very little Na<sub>3</sub>Sb/a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb remains in the electrode at full desodiation. Baggetto et al. reason that the isomeric shift observed for antimony at the end of charge is shifted from crystalline antimony due to residual sodium present in the material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> The increase to the c-lattice parameter of the desodiated c-Sb phase compared to the pristine electrode (11.38(4) vs 11.27(1) Å, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>), seems too small for the c-Sb to contain any significant amount of sodium. It is more likely that sodium is trapped in the SEI and within the amorphous component of the electrode that remains in a stoichiometry of Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb, the latter in turn preventing the crystallization of this part of the electrode.</div></div><div id="sec3_2_3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_4"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h5 class="article-section__title" id="_i15">3.2.3.3 Second Sodiation</h5><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemistry of the second sodiation differs significantly from the first sodiation. A total capacity of 544 mAhg<sup>–1</sup> is observed in four distinct voltage processes indicating that several sequential sodiation reactions take place. Careful analysis of the PDF data allows the structural changes associated with each electrochemical signature to be isolated: the crystalline and amorphous electrode components were again separated by refining the crystalline structures using high-<i>r</i> (20–50 Å) data and the information from these refinements then used to constrain the refinements using the full data set and extract the amorphous component. The changes in the PDFs for the two components are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">During S2-a, 0.64 Na per Sb are inserted into the electrode. There is little change to the PDF at high-<i>r</i> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, right), indicating that crystalline Sb does not take part in sodiation reactions at this voltage. However, the PDF for the amorphous component of the electrode shows considerable change at this voltage (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, left); the peaks at distances between 2.85 and 9 Å lower in intensity signifying breakdown of the amorphous antimony network. The PDF of the amorphous component at the end of S2-a contains features that are similar to that of a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb, formed during D1-a (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S27</a> compares these two PDFs), consistent with <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra that show the reappearance of the broad resonance at 27 ppm, ascribed in the previous section to sodium within a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb. Therefore, we propose that the S2-a process is characteristic of the breakup of the amorphous antimony network (a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb) to reform a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb.</div><div class="NLM_p">0.56 Na per Sb are inserted into the electrode during S2-b. High-<i>r</i> peaks in the PDF disappear in this region as the nanocrystalline Sb component of the electrode is broken down (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, right) to form amorphous phases; the reaction does not result in any additional crystalline phases. The PDF for the amorphous phases grows (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The 2.85 Å Sb–Sb correlation remains almost constant, but a defined shoulder develops on the high-<i>r</i> side of this peak 3.44 Å, accompanied by the appearance of an additional peak at around 5.5 Å. Both peaks are characteristic of a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb indicating that this phase begins to form during S1-b.</div><div class="NLM_p">Linear combinations of the PDFs of a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb (taken from the PDF for this phase extracted at the end of D1-a) and a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb (taken from the PDF at the end of S2-c), (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>), indicate that c-Sb does not form the dumbbell phase Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb: the scale factor for the Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb remains constant during S2-b, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> while that for the a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb increases linearly. This would indicate that the c-Sb reacts straight to form a Na<sub>3</sub>Sb-like phase, probably due to the structural similarity between the two phases discussed earlier. At the end of S2-b, the PDF can be modeled as a linear combination of the PDFs of a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb with phase fractions of 0.53 and 0.47, respectively (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>). At this stage, a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb is still the dominant phase in the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra, and the weaker resonance from a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb at 37 ppm must be buried under that of the a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb resonance. We note that the relative amounts of a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb are likely to be closely related to the exact amount of c-Sb that is reformed at the height of charge and may explain why very little is produced in some samples, and subtle differences between in situ and ex situ samples may also result in differences to the phase fractions obtained in regions where metastable phases are dominant.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Results of linear combination fitting of the PDFs extracted during processes S2-b and S2-c with PDFs for a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb (obtained from D1-a) and a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb (from the end of D2-c). Top: Example of the fit of the linear combination (orange line) with experimental data (gray circles). The difference between the experimental data and the fit is shown offset in gray. The contribution of a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb (blue solid line) and a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb (green dashed line) is shown offset below. Bottom: Variation of the scale factors for a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb (blue circles) and a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb (orange squares) with sodiation level.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The plateau at 450 mV, S2-c, is reminiscent of the S1-a process that dominates the first sodiation. 0.45 Na per Sb are inserted into the electrode and peaks are observed in the PDF to higher-<i>r</i> (20 Å), indicating that a more ordered structure is formed. The 2.85 Å peak in the PDF disappears during this process, and the peak at 3.1 Å becomes the dominant low-<i>r</i> peak, signifying that Sb–Sb bonds are broken during this region to form a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb. A single-phase a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb electrode, where <i>x</i> ≈ 0.5 based on the stoichiometry calculated from electrochemical measurements, is formed at the end of S2-c. A starting model based on c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb structure was refined against the PDF data obtained at the end of S2-c, using very large thermal parameters to capture the disorder in the material and a spherical particle envelope to capture the limited correlation length in the material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Sodium occupancies were constrained to be 0.83, based on the sodiation level calculated from the electrochemical measurements. The refinement is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S28</a> and structural parameters are found in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S9</a>. A spherical particle diameter of 21.7 Å was obtained from the refinement. The structure that is obtained from this refinement comprises of hexagonal layers of alternating Na and Sb, similar on a local scale to c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, but with more closely spaced layer evidenced by the decrease in c-lattice parameter from 9.49(1) Å to 9.19(1) Å, presumably due to sodium vacancies between the layers. This is consistent with <sup>23</sup>Na NMR results that indicate a more Sb-rich sodium environment in this phase compared to c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. Thermal parameters for both the antimony and sodium atoms in the interplanar direction are very large, which indicates significant turbostratic disorder of sodium within the structure; <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> a large number of sodium environments are likely to exist within a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb, a result that is consistent with the broad peak observed in the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra for this species. This approach captures most of the features in the PDF and allows us to propose that the local structure of the a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb is very similar to the c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb structure, and the similarity of both c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb to c-Sb discussed above is likely to make this reaction pathway kinetically facile.</div><div class="NLM_p">The PDF results indicate that a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb exists in isolation at the end of S2-c, but despite several attempts to obtain an ex situ sample containing pure a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb, none were successful. The presence of small amounts of c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb in all ex situ spectra where a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb is present is ascribed to the metastability of the a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb; once removed from the battery, some of the material may react to form c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and other reaction products appearing overlapped in the SEI region. The exact proportion of the two phases is likely to depend on the exact sodiation at the point where the cell is stopped.</div><div class="NLM_p last">During S2-d, the correlation length of the electrode increases as c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is formed from a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb. c–Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is the final product of the second sodiation. The structural parameters after refinement against PDF data are not significantly different from the end of the first sodiation (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>). The <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra at the end of the second sodiation confirm c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb as the final product.</div></div><div id="sec3_2_3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_4"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h5 class="article-section__title" id="_i16">3.2.2.4 Further Quantification of the Na Phases Present During the First Sodiation</h5><div class="NLM_p last">The model for a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb obtained from refinement against data at the end of S2-c was used to model the a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb formed during the first sodiation. Using the structural parameters for c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and c-Sb obtained from two-phase least-squares refinements against the PDF data at high-<i>r</i>, refinements were performed using the full <i>r</i>-range (2–50 Å), using a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb as an additional phase, refining only the scale factors for the three phases. This approach improves the fit to data in the intermediate region between 0.6–2.8 Na per Sb (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>, bottom); <i>R</i><sub><i>w</i></sub> improve in all cases, but particularly in the intermediate region where the sodiation level is between 1.8 and 3 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S6</a>). The approach also improves the fit of the sodiation level expected from structural data with that obtained from the electrochemical measurements (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>, top). Some deviation is still observed at low sodiation levels, because of surface processes (reaction with the carbon) and SEI formation that take place early on in the sodiation process and to which the PDF is not sensitive.</div></div></div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17">3.3 Density Functional Theory (DFT) Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Density-functional theory calculations were performed on structures generated by our species-swapping method (see section <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">SI</a>.4 for details of the method). The only stable structures found on a zero temperature convex hull (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) were NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb having <i>P</i>2<sub>1</sub>/<i>c</i> and <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> symmetries, respectively. At low sodiation, only 0.009 eV/formula unit (f.u.) above the hull tie-line is a NaSb<sub>2</sub><i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> phase. The structure comprises Sb sheets broken by Na atoms, showing that Sb–Sb bonds must be broken even at low Na content, producing structures that are not simply Na intercalated Sb; thus c-Sb is not easy to sodiate, consistent with the experimental results. A 6 formula unit per cell Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase with <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>cm symmetry was found only 0.018 eV/f.u. above the ground state <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> structure. This indicates that for the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb stoichiometry at room temperature the system will easily explore lower symmetry and larger repeat-length structures; i.e., there are many low-energy amorphous structures. The conversion of c-Sb to c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is predicted to be at 0.57 V in good agreement with experiment. There are no other obvious thermally accessible phases in the species-swapped convex hull diagram, indicating that any additional phases are likely to be metastable phases, in agreement with the results of Saubanère et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a></div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i18">4 Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i19" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i19">4.1 1st Sodiation: c-Sb → a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb + c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb → c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The first sodiation of antimony is dominated by the breakdown of the crystalline antimony electrode. The overpotential required to breakdown the crystalline lattice results a single pseudo-plateau at approximately 500 mV. Both NMR and PDF data indicate that amorphous Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb is the major product during S1-a, though some c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is formed concurrently as a result of the overpotential. However, significant formation of c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb only occurs once the majority of the crystalline antimony has been broken down into Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb, indicating there are kinetic difficulties associated with nucleating crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb) while Sb–Sb bonds are still present in the material. This is reminiscent of lithium-alloying systems including Li–Si and Li–Ge, where amorphous phases are formed during the breakage of dumbbell phases, and crystalline Li<sub>15</sub>Si<sub>4</sub> and Li<sub>15</sub>Ge<sub>4</sub> only form when the Si–Si or Ge–Ge bonds are essentially all broken. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref31" aria-label="reference 22 and 31">(22, 31)</a> DFT calculations indicate that lower symmetry c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb-like structures with more disordered sodium sites can easily be formed, indicating a propensity of this structure to disorder, and therefore to accommodate some under/over stoichiometry. We observe no evidence that environments similar to NaSb are formed, in contrast to the results of Baggetto et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> However, we do note that the overpotential on the first sodiation is likely to be highly dependent on the particle size and formulation (film thickness, additives, etc.) and therefore the mechanism for very thin films on the first sodiation may differ from what is presented here. The final product of sodiation is confirmed to be c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra for c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb indicate high sodium mobility in this structure, and structural refinements against PDF data indicate some inherent disorder in the material. Potentiostatic intermittent titration technique (PITT) measurements reported previously indicate the sodium diffusion coefficient is high (3–4 times larger than the lithium diffusion coefficient in cubic Li<sub>3</sub>Sb) close to 0 V. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a></div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i20" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i20">4.2 1st Desodiation: c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb → a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb → a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb → a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb + c-Sb</h3><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of the NMR and PDF experiments reported here, we suggest that a different pathway is taken on desodiation. A 0.2 V difference between sodiation and desodiation curves determined from galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) experiments are consistent with this hypothesis. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> The formation of Sb–Sb bonding immediately on desodiation is apparent from the peak at 2.85 Å, which appears in the PDF during D1-a; no resonance corresponding to a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb is observed in the NMR, ruling out the reformation of a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb. Instead, the good fit of a linear combination of c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb to the PDFs collected during D1-a indicates that a two-phase reaction between c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb, the latter a highly amorphous phase containing Sb-dumbbell units and no significant ordering of sodium, takes place. These results are in contrast to those reported by Baggetto et al., who proposed the reformation of the same intermediate formed on sodiation of thin films. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">We ascribe the different pathway taken on desodiation to poor kinetics in the electrode; variable temperature NMR experiments have indicated that the sodium mobility in c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb is high, and therefore its removal on initial desodiation is should be facile. Presumably the limited antimony mobility prevents crystallization of the thermodynamically stable NaSb phase, and Sb-dimers, instead of more extended Sb–Sb networks, are initially formed. DFT calculations indicate that no additional thermodynamically stable phases exist within the Na–Sb phase diagram, indicating that the pathway must occur via a kinetically-favored structural motif. The formation of dimers when the Na:Sb ratio is close to 2:1 is unsurprising since Zintl counting rules predict that the antimony should be present with a formal charge of Sb<sup>2–</sup>; this results in the same connectivity found in halogen molecules, i.e., X<sub>2</sub>, and dimers are to be expected. Future experiments to probe the effect of temperature on the phases formed are planned, but it is clear from both NMR and PDF results that in room-temperature batteries, the NaSb phase is not formed on either sodiation or desodiation.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Kinetics also dictate the structure formed on further desodiation. As more sodium is removed, the antimony connectivity increases to form a network, similar to that reported for amorphous antimony. In areas where additional sodium can be removed the electrode forms crystalline antimony, but with a significantly reduced correlation length indicating it may be present as nanoparticles. It is not clear whether complete Na removal (from the amorphous phase) is prevented due to difficulties in Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the amorphous phase, or whether the (kinetic) formation of the amorphous phase hinders further crystallization of the Sb phase again due to the difficulty of breaking and reforming Sb bonds.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i21" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i21">4.3 2nd Sodiation: a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb + c-Sb → a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb + c-Sb → a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb + a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb → a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb → c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb</h3><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of PDF results, the different electrochemical profile observed on the second sodiation can be attributed to two key factors both intimately connected to the complex structure of the electrode formed on desodiation. First, the amorphous and crystalline phases sodiate at different voltages, something that is clearly observed in the PDF data. Antimony contained as amorphous a-Na<sub>1.0</sub>Sb reacts during S2-a to reform a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb. Crystalline antimony does not react until lower voltages (S2-b) and, significantly, it appears that it reacts straight to the a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb phase without forming the Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb phase, similar to the behavior observed on the first sodiation. Second, the correlation length/particle size and crystallinity of the c-Sb that is present at the start of the second sodiation is reduced compared to the pristine starting material. As a result of this, the overpotential required to break down the crystalline lattice that is responsible for the single plateau observed in the first sodiation, is reduced or removed entirely on the second sodiation, and as a result of this additional plateaus (S2-b/c) can be resolved in the electrochemistry.</div><div class="NLM_p">On the basis of the structural studies outlined here, we can propose that several structural factors are responsible for the excellent cycling and rate performance of antimony, in addition to those factors discussed by previous authors–i.e., the good electrical conductivity and the low packing density of the antimony structure that promotes ion movement through the structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref6" aria-label="reference 3 and 6">(3, 6)</a> First, we show that the two intermediates, a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb are highly amorphous structures; this reduces the strain associated with multiple phase transitions. Second, PDF measurements show the formation of a complex electrode containing both amorphous and crystalline antimony networks at full desodiation. The different reaction voltages of these components lead to a more sequential reaction profile, where an inactive component can, to some extent, accommodate the strain resulting from reactions in other phases of the electrode, thus preventing harmful side processes that lead to capacity fade. Lastly, the high sodium mobility within the final c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb structure is likely to contribute to the good rate performance of antimony by increasing sodium movement in and out of the phase leading to facile structural transformations.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Finally, we note that examination of the second sodiation process is vital, not only for understanding the structure of the “active” electrode material - which in this case differs considerably from the single-phase pristine electrode–and, therefore, the (de)sodiation mechanism of antimony in real systems, but also for understanding the complex processes going on in the electrode that can be masked by the presence of an overpotential in the first sodiation.</div></div></div><div id="sec5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i22">5 Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Using in-depth analysis of the PDF data, constrained by structural and chemical information from known model Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases and <sup>23</sup>Na ssNMR, we are able to demonstrate, for the first time, the separation of amorphous and crystalline phases formed in a sodium-ion battery and link structural information to all electrochemical signatures observed, allowing a comprehensive mechanism of (de)sodiation to be assembled (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>). Operando PDF analysis of sodium-ion batteries, when constrained by chemical information from <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectroscopy, referenced to known model compounds NaSb and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, was used to separate the amorphous and crystalline structures formed in antimony anodes during (de)sodiation. This approach has led to the identification of previously unknown amorphous intermediate phases a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb and a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb and tracking of their interconversion within a sodium-ion battery. We propose that a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb (0.4 &lt; <i>x</i> &lt; 0.5) is locally similar to crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>Sb, with hexagonal layers of antimony interspersed with sodium, but with a reduced interlayer spacing resulting from significant numbers of Na-vacancies between the Sb-layers. The PDF for a-Na<sub>1.7</sub>Sb has a single sharp peak at 2.85 Å indicative of a Sb-dumbbell motif present within the highly amorphous structure. Inclusion of amorphous phases into real-space least-squares refinements results in a better match of the sodium level calculated from structural refinements with that obtained from electrochemical measurements, far extending previously known mechanistic details.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/medium/ja-2015-13273h_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. PDF and NMR-derived mechanism of (de)sodiation of antimony from the first desodiation during galvanostatic cycling at a rate of C/20.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/asset/images/large/ja-2015-13273h_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.5b13273&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The structural origins of the different electrochemical profiles observed on the first and second sodiations are explored; (de)sodiation processes during the first cycle result in a change to the active electrode from crystalline antimony to a composite electrode containing both amorphous and crystalline antimony networks at full desodiation. The different reaction voltages of the amorphous and crystalline components of the electrode are responsible for the additional processes observed in subsequent cycles. <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectroscopy highlights the anomalously high sodium mobility within the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb final sodiation product, a likely contributing factor to the exceptional rate performance of antimony compared to other alloying anodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">We show that the sodiation of antimony is controlled to a large degree by the kinetics of the system. DFT calculations show no additional thermodynamically stable phases exist on the zero temperature convex hull. Formation of undersodiated Na<sub>3</sub>Sb (a-Na<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub>Sb) takes place due to difficulties in nucleating c-Na<sub>3</sub>Sb in regions where Sb–Sb bonds must still be broken. The absence of NaSb in both sodiation and desodiation is also ascribed to the lack of mobility of Sb within the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phases, particularly after the initial Sb–Sb bonds (in the dumbbells) have already formed, instead forming amorphous structure containing first Sb-dumbbells and later extended, but disordered, networks of antimony.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrode formed after desodiation is a composite of amorphous and crystalline antimony networks. We show that these connectivities react at distinct voltages via different sodiation pathways; the sequential manner of these transformations enhances the cyclability of the electrode by having an “inactive” component capable of buffering of strain associated with multiple phase transitions.</div><div class="NLM_p">We highlight the role that connectivity plays in determining the sodiation pathway in kinetically controlled systems; here, amorphous and crystalline antimony networks react through different intermediate phases that are structurally related to the starting connectivity. This result could have wider implications for understanding other kinetically limited alloying systems where the starting connectivity is likely to have a large effect on the pathway taken and the capacities observed. For example, germanium nanowire anodes for sodium-ion batteries show little capacity in their crystalline form, but high capacity and good rate performance when prelithiated and amorphised. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Our results have additional implications for understanding high-rate nanostructured antimony anodes, because the electrochemical processes observed in bulk antimony are also observed in nanostructured systems beyond the first sodiation, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 6 to 11">(6-11)</a> indicating that the same antimony-based phase transitions take place. Study of bulk antimony as a “model” system helps to decouple the processes associated with surface oxide groups with those associated with Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb phase transitions in such nanostructured electrodes.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i23">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/jacs.5b13273" class="ext-link">10.1021/jacs.5b13273</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Full PDF data, further details about XRD and PDF analysis, DFT calculations and 2D NMR results. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.5b13273/suppl_file/ja5b13273_si_001.pdf">ja5b13273_si_001.pdf (4.61 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.5b13273" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Intrinsic thermodynamic and kinetic properties of Sb electrodes for Li-ion and Na-ion batteries: experiment and theory</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Baggetto, Loic; Ganesh, P.; Sun, Che-Nan; Meisner, Roberta A.; Zawodzinski, Thomas A.; Veith, Gabriel M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">27</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7985-7994</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A detailed comparative study between the electrochem. lithiation and sodiation of pure Sb, relating changes in structural, thermodn., kinetic and electrochem. properties was carried out.  For this purpose, a wide range of measurements using electrochem. (galvanostatic cycling, GITT, PITT), XRD and XPS methods as well as d. functional theory (DFT) based studies have been undertaken.  Assessment of the thermodn. reveals that the reaction proceeds identically during the 1st and 2nd cycles for Li whereas it differs between the 1st and subsequent cycles for Na as the reaction with Na proceeds through a different pathway assocd. with the formation of amorphous NaxSb phases.  For the 1st time the authors rationalize the amorphization of NaxSb phases by the long ranged strain propagation due to Na-vacancy compared to Li-Sb.  At full discharge, the XRD results show that a minor fraction of hexagonal Li3Sb forms concomitantly with cubic Li3Sb.  The XRD results confirm that Sb crystallizes into hexagonal Na3Sb at full sodiation.  The kinetics of the reaction is assessed by rate performance tests which highlight that both Li and Na can diffuse rapidly throughout micron thick films at room temp.  However, the (de)insertion of Li provides lower overpotentials and larger storage capacities compared to Na.  The difference in rate performance is complemented by diffusion coeff. detns. near the 0 V region where both materials are crystd. into M3Sb (M = Li, Na).  Calcns. show that the energy barrier for near-neighbor vacancy migration, predominant in these close-packed phases, is about twice for Na than for Li.  The anal. tries to relate the lower intrinsic diffusivity of Na compared to Li with the long-range strain propagation induced by the former, thereby leading to an intrinsic origin of differences in rates, mech. properties and amorphization.  Finally, the surface chem. of Sb electrodes cycled in NaClO4 dissolved in pure PC with(out) the addn. of 5% EC or FEC shows presence of ethers and NaF for the EC- and FEC-based electrolytes, resp., and SEI films rich in Na-based carbonates.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/nl404165c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">He, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kravchyk, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Walter, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kovalenko, M. V.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">14</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1255</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A New Class of Lithium and Sodium Rechargeable Batteries Based on Selenium and Selenium-Sulfur as a Positive Electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Abouimrane, Ali; Dambournet, Damien; Chapman, Karena W.; Chupas, Peter J.; Weng, Wei; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">134</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4505-4508</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A new class of selenium and selenium-sulfur (SexSy)-based cathode materials for room temp. lithium and sodium batteries is reported.  The structural mechanisms for Li/Na insertion in these electrodes were investigated using pair distribution function anal.  Not only does the Se electrode show promising electrochem. performance with both Li and Na anodes, but the addnl. potential for mixed SexSy systems allows for tunable electrodes, combining the high capacities of S-rich systems with the high elec. cond. of the d-electron contg. Se.  Unlike the widely studied Li/S system, both Se and SexSy can be cycled to high voltages (up to 4.6 V) without failure.  Their high densities and voltage output offer greater volumetric energy densities than S-based batteries, opening possibilities for new energy storage systems that can enable elec. vehicles and smart grids.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1021/ic2004326"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dambournet, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chapman, K.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">RMCProfile: reverse Monte Carlo for polycrystalline materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tucker, Matthew G.; Keen, David A.; Dove, Martin T.; Goodwin, Andrew L.; Hui, Qun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physics: Condensed Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2007</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">19</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">335218/1-335218/16</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCOMEL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0953-8984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute of Physics Publishing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A new approach to the reverse Monte Carlo anal. of total scattering data from polycryst. materials is presented.  The essential new feature is the incorporation of an explicit anal. of the Bragg peaks using a profile refinement, taking account of the instrument resoln. function.  Other new features including fitting data from magnetic materials, modeling lattice site disorder, and new restraint and constraint options.  The new method is demonstrated by a brief review of studies carried out during its development.  The new program RMCProfile represents a significant advance in the anal. of polycryst. total scattering data, esp. where the local structure is to be explored within the true constraints of the long-range av. structure.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1088/0953-8984/19/33/335219"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Farrow, C.                 10.1088/0953-8984/19/33/335219</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DFarrow%252C%2BC.%2BL.%253B%2BJuhas%252C%2BP.%253B%2BLiu%252C%2BJ.%2BW.%253B%2BBryndin%252C%2BD.%253B%2BBo%25C5%25BEin%252C%2BE.%2BS.%253B%2BBloch%252C%2BJ.%253B%2BProffen%252C%2BT.%253B%2BBillinge%252C%2BS.%2BJ.%2BL.J.%2BPhys.%253A%2BCondens.%2BMatter%2B2007%252C%2B19%252C%2B335219&amp;doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.5b13273&amp;doiOfLink=10.1088%2F0953-8984%2F19%2F33%2F335219&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">PDFfit2 and PDFgui: computer programs for studying nanostructure in crystals</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Farrow, C. L.; Juhas, P.; Liu, J. W.; Bryndin, D.; Bozin, E. S.; Bloch, J.; Proffen, Th; Billinge, S. J. L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physics: Condensed Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2007</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">19</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">335219/1-335219/7</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCOMEL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0953-8984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute of Physics Publishing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">PDFfit2 is a program as well as a library for real-space refinement of crystal structures.  It is capable of fitting a theor. three-dimensional (3D) structure to at. pair distribution function data and is ideal for nanoscale investigations.  The fit system accounts for lattice consts., at. positions and anisotropic at. displacement parameters, correlated at. motion, and exptl. factors that may affect the data.  The at. positions and thermal coeffs. can be constrained to follow the symmetry requirements of an arbitrary space group.  The PDFfit2 engine is written in C++ and is accessible via Python, allowing it to inter-operate with other Python programs.  PDFgui is a graphical interface built on the PDFfit2 engine.  PDFgui organizes fits and simplifies many data anal. tasks, such as configuring and plotting multiple fits.  PDFfit2 and PDFgui are freely available via the Internet.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.72.104204"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Soper, A.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Phys.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Applications of an amorphous silicon-based area detector for high-resolution, high-sensitivity and fast time-resolved pair distribution function measurements</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chupas, Peter J.; Chapman, Karena W.; Lee, Peter L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Applied Crystallography</span>

                        cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revealing lithium-silicide phase transformations in nano-structured silicon-based lithium ion batteries via in situ NMR spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ogata K; Hofmann S; Salager E; Kerr C J; Fraser A E; Ducati C; Morris A J; Grey C P</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3217</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Nano-structured silicon anodes are attractive alternatives to graphitic carbons in rechargeable Li-ion batteries, owing to their extremely high capacities.  Despite their advantages, numerous issues remain to be addressed, the most basic being to understand the complex kinetics and thermodynamics that control the reactions and structural rearrangements.  Elucidating this necessitates real-time in situ metrologies, which are highly challenging, if the whole electrode structure is studied at an atomistic level for multiple cycles under realistic cycling conditions.  Here we report that Si nanowires grown on a conducting carbon-fibre support provide a robust model battery system that can be studied by (7)Li in situ NMR spectroscopy.  The method allows the (de)alloying reactions of the amorphous silicides to be followed in the 2nd cycle and beyond.  In combination with density-functional theory calculations, the results provide insight into the amorphous and amorphous-to-crystalline lithium-silicide transformations, particularly those at low voltages, which are highly relevant to practical cycling strategies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1021/ja108085d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Key, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Morcrette, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.-M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Grey, C. Electrochem. measurements confirm that the reaction path followed during Li insertion into Sb electrodes differs from that followed upon subsequent Li extn.  Results from first-principles calcns. and NMR measurements indicate that Li3Sb is capable of tolerating high Li-vacancy concns.  An unusually high Li mobility in Li3Sb facilitates over potentials during charging, which leads to a substantially larger driving force for the nucleation of Sb compared to that of Li2Sb.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The structural changes in Si, electrochem. lithiated and de-lithiated at room temp., were studied by x-ray powder diffraction.  Cryst.                         cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Local structure of nanoporous carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Petkov, V.; DiFrancesco, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The local at. structure of nanoporous carbons produced by pyrolysis of poly(furfuryl alc.) at various temps. has been studied using neutron diffraction.  At. pair distribution functions (PDFs) were obtained from the neutron data.  Structure models have been fitted to the PDFs to understand the fine features of at. ordering.  It has been found that carbons produced at 800 and 1200°C are made of stacked graphene sheets, that are more or less curved.  The 400°C-processed carbon has a heavily distorted nonplanar structure.  For each sample we have detd. the extent of the structural coherence, the av. d. within the structurally coherent regions and the proportion of carbon atoms, which are trigonally coordinated.  Microscopic models were also produced for the local structure of the nanoporous carbons investigated.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref47" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2015.01.093"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Saubanère, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yahia, M.-B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lemoigno, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Doublet, M.-L.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                 10.1039/c3ta11568b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Intrinsic thermodynamic and kinetic properties of Sb electrodes for Li-ion and Na-ion batteries: experiment and theory</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Baggetto, Loic; Ganesh, P.; Sun, Che-Nan; Meisner, Roberta A.; Zawodzinski, Thomas A.; Veith, Gabriel M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">27</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7985-7994</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A detailed comparative study between the electrochem. lithiation and sodiation of pure Sb, relating changes in structural, thermodn., kinetic and electrochem. properties was carried out.  For this purpose, a wide range of measurements using electrochem. (galvanostatic cycling, GITT, PITT), XRD and XPS methods as well as d. functional theory (DFT) based studies have been undertaken.  Assessment of the thermodn. reveals that the reaction proceeds identically during the 1st and 2nd cycles for Li whereas it differs between the 1st and subsequent cycles for Na as the reaction with Na proceeds through a different pathway assocd. with the formation of amorphous NaxSb phases.  For the 1st time the authors rationalize the amorphization of NaxSb phases by the long ranged strain propagation due to Na-vacancy compared to Li-Sb.  At full discharge, the XRD results show that a minor fraction of hexagonal Li3Sb forms concomitantly with cubic Li3Sb.  The XRD results confirm that Sb crystallizes into hexagonal Na3Sb at full sodiation.  The kinetics of the reaction is assessed by rate performance tests which highlight that both Li and Na can diffuse rapidly throughout micron thick films at room temp.  However, the (de)insertion of Li provides lower overpotentials and larger storage capacities compared to Na.  The difference in rate performance is complemented by diffusion coeff. detns. near the 0 V region where both materials are crystd. into M3Sb (M = Li, Na).  Calcns. show that the energy barrier for near-neighbor vacancy migration, predominant in these close-packed phases, is about twice for Na than for Li.  The anal. tries to relate the lower intrinsic diffusivity of Na compared to Li with the long-range strain propagation induced by the former, thereby leading to an intrinsic origin of differences in rates, mech. properties and amorphization.  Finally, the surface chem. of Sb electrodes cycled in NaClO4 dissolved in pure PC with(out) the addn. of 5% EC or FEC shows presence of ethers and NaF for the EC- and FEC-based electrolytes, resp., and SEI films rich in Na-based carbonates.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1021/nl404165c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">He, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kravchyk, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Walter, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kovalenko, M. V.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">14</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1255</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A New Class of Lithium and Sodium Rechargeable Batteries Based on Selenium and Selenium-Sulfur as a Positive Electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Abouimrane, Ali; Dambournet, Damien; Chapman, Karena W.; Chupas, Peter J.; Weng, Wei; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">134</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">4505-4508</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A new class of selenium and selenium-sulfur (SexSy)-based cathode materials for room temp. lithium and sodium batteries is reported.  The structural mechanisms for Li/Na insertion in these electrodes were investigated using pair distribution function anal.  Not only does the Se electrode show promising electrochem. performance with both Li and Na anodes, but the addnl. potential for mixed SexSy systems allows for tunable electrodes, combining the high capacities of S-rich systems with the high elec. cond. of the d-electron contg. Se.  Unlike the widely studied Li/S system, both Se and SexSy can be cycled to high voltages (up to 4.6 V) without failure.  Their high densities and voltage output offer greater volumetric energy densities than S-based batteries, opening possibilities for new energy storage systems that can enable elec. vehicles and smart grids.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1021/ic2004326"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dambournet, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chapman, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A new approach to the reverse Monte Carlo anal. of total scattering data from polycryst. materials is presented.         </div><div class="casAbstract">PDFfit2 is a program as well as a library for real-space refinement of crystal structures.  It is capable of fitting a theor. three-dimensional (3D) structure to at. pair distribution function data and is ideal for nanoscale investigations.  The fit system accounts for lattice consts., at. positions and anisotropic at. displacement parameters, correlated at. motion, and exptl. factors that may affect the data.  The at. positions and thermal coeffs. can be constrained to follow the symmetry requirements of an arbitrary space group.  The PDFfit2 engine is written in C++ and is accessible via Python, allowing it to inter-operate with other Python programs.  PDFgui is a graphical interface built on the PDFfit2 engine.  PDFgui organizes fits and simplifies many data anal. tasks, such as configuring and plotting multiple fits.  PDFfit2 and PDFgui are freely available via the Internet.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.72.104204"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Soper, A.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Phys.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The application of a large-area (41 × 41 cm, 2048 × 2048 or 1024 × 1024 pixel) high-sensitivity (detective quantum efficiency &gt; 65%) fast-readout (up to 7.5 or 30 Hz) flat-panel detector based on an amorphous Si array system to the collection of high-energy x-ray scattering data for quant. pair distribution function (PDF) anal. is evaluated and discussed.                         rightTab-cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Revealing lithium-silicide phase transformations in nano-structured silicon-based lithium ion batteries via in situ NMR spectroscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ogata K; Hofmann S; Salager E; Kerr C J; Fraser A E; Ducati C; Morris A J; Grey C P</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3217</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Nano-structured silicon anodes are attractive alternatives to graphitic carbons in rechargeable Li-ion batteries, owing to their extremely high capacities.  Despite their advantages, numerous issues remain to be addressed, the most basic being to understand the complex kinetics and thermodynamics that control the reactions and structural rearrangements.  Elucidating this necessitates real-time in situ metrologies, which are highly challenging, if the whole electrode structure is studied at an atomistic level for multiple cycles under realistic cycling conditions.  Here we report that Si nanowires grown on a conducting carbon-fibre support provide a robust model battery system that can be studied by (7)Li in situ NMR spectroscopy.  The method allows the (de)alloying reactions of the amorphous silicides to be followed in the 2nd cycle and beyond.  In combination with density-functional theory calculations, the results provide insight into the amorphous and amorphous-to-crystalline lithium-silicide transformations, particularly those at low voltages, which are highly relevant to practical cycling strategies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1021/ja108085d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Key, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Morcrette, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.-M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Grey, C.
